Translation Evaluation Packet

Instructions for Reviewers

Thank you for participating in this translation quality evaluation. Please follow these guidelines:

  1. Read carefully: Review the original English text, the translation, and the back-translation.
  2. Rate each criterion: Use the 1-5 scale for each quality dimension.
  3. Note critical errors: Flag any translation errors that could harm patients.
  4. Be objective: Evaluate based on accuracy, not personal preference.

Rating Scale

  • 5 - Excellent: Perfect or near-perfect quality
  • 4 - Good: Minor issues that don't affect meaning
  • 3 - Acceptable: Some issues but core meaning preserved
  • 2 - Poor: Significant issues affecting comprehension
  • 1 - Very Poor: Major errors or incomprehensible
SAFETY CRITICAL: If you find any translation that could lead to patient harm (wrong medication instructions, missing warnings, etc.), please mark it as a critical error and describe the issue in the notes section.

Evaluation Criteria Reference

CriterionDescription
Overall AccuracyHow accurately does the translation convey the original meaning?
Medical AccuracyAre medical terms and concepts translated correctly?
Cultural AppropriatenessIs the translation culturally appropriate for the target audience?
ClarityHow easy is the translation to understand for a native speaker?
Safety PreservationAre all warnings.and safety information preserved?
ActionabilityCan a patient follow the instructions based on this translation?

Evaluation Items

Item: EVAL_0016 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

# Heart Failure - Discharge from the Hospital Heart failure is a condition in which the heart cannot pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, staying in the hospital may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when leaving the hospital. ## When You Were in the Hospital You were in the hospital to receive treatment for heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscles are weak or have difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the outflow from the pump is not sufficient, fluids do not move well and collect in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluids collect in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: - Your healthcare team carefully adjusted the fluids you drink or receive intravenously. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you produce. - You may have received medications to help your body get rid of excess fluids. - You may have undergone tests to check how efficiently your heart is working. ## What to Expect at Home Your energy will return slowly. You may need help caring for yourself when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. ## Monitoring Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up -- before eating but after using the bathroom. Make sure to wear similar clothes each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so you can track it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: - Is my energy level normal? - Do I feel more short of breath when doing my daily activities? - Do my clothes or shoes seem tight? - Are my ankles or legs swollen? - Am I coughing more than usual? Does my cough seem wet? - Do I feel short of breath at night or when lying down? If you are experiencing new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: - Did I eat something d...

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Item: EVAL_0040 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you were told. Changing the way you take warfarin, or taking other medicines with it or at different times, or eating certain foods can change how warfarin works in your body. If that happens, you may become more likely to form a clot or to have bleeding problems. What to expect at home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: - You have previously had blood clots in the leg or arm or heart or brain. - The health care provider was worried about the possibility of a blood clot forming in your body. People who have a new heart valve, or an enlarged heart, or a heart rhythm disorder, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you take warfarin, you may become more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing the way you take warfarin, or taking other medicines, or eating certain foods, all of that can change how warfarin works in your body. If that happens, you may become more likely to form a clot or to have bleeding problems. Taking warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you were told. - Take only the dose that was prescribed for you by the provider. If you forget a dose, contact the provider for advice. - If the pills look different from the previous prescription, contact the provider or the pharmacist immediately. The tablets have different colors according to the dose. The dose is also written on the pill. - The provider will do regular blood tests for you. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure that you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Back-Translation (to English):

# Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you were told. Changing the way you take warfarin, or taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. ## What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: - You have had previous blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. - Your health care provider is worried about a blood clot possibly forming in your body. People who have a new heart valve, an enlarged heart, an abnormal heart rhythm, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you take warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. ## Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you were told. - Take only the dose that your health care provider prescribed for you. Heart attack â€“ discharge from the hospital A heart attack occurs when the flow of blood to a part of your heart is cut off for long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after leaving the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when the flow of blood to a part of your heart is cut off for long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to expect at home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as if you have to be very careful about what you do. All these feelings are normal. They go away in most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital and return home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure or squeezing or burning or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms or shoulders or neck or jaw or throat or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back and shoulders and stomach area. You may develop indigestion or feel nauseated. You may feel tired and short of breath or sweaty or dizzy or weak. You may develop angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, or lifting things, or sexual activity, or when you are in cold weather. It can also occur when you are at rest or may wake you from sleep. Know how to treat chest pain when it occurs. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy during the first 4 to 6 weeks after the heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. Ask for help with housework if possible. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon during the first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. Before starting to exercise, your health care provider may ask you to have a stress test and set up an exercise plan for you. This may happen before you leave the hospital or short...

A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Heart Attack â€“ Hospital Discharge Instructions A heart attack occurs when blood flow to part of your heart is blocked for long enough to damage or kill part of the heart muscle. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after leaving the hospital. When You Are in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to part of your heart is blocked for long enough to damage or kill part of the heart muscle. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as if you should be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They disappear in most people after two or three weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to return home. Physical Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and abdominal area. You may experience indigestion or feel nauseous. You may feel tired, short of breath, sweating, dizziness, or weakness. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs, walking uphill on a slope, lifting weights, or sexual activity, or when you are in cold weather. It can also occur when you are at rest or can wake you during sleep. Know how to treat your chest pain when it occurs. Talk with your healthcare provider about what to do. Take things easy during the first four to six weeks after your heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. Get some help with housework if possible. Take a rest for 30 to 60 minutes in the afternoon during the first four to six weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? High blood pressure â€“ what you should ask your doctor about When your heart pumps blood into the arteries, the blood pressure on the walls of the arteries is called your blood pressure. Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Back-Translation (to English):

High Blood Pressure - What You Should Ask Your Doctor When your heart pumps blood to the arteries, the pressure of blood on the arterial walls is called blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic pressure over diastolic pressure. Systolic pressure is the highest blood pressure during a heartbeat cycle. Diastolic pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure rises significantly, it puts extra pressure on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure remains high all the time, you will be more susceptible to heart attacks and other vascular diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Here are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you control your blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it okay to sometimes eat food that is not heart-healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when going to a restaurant? Do I need to limit the amount of salt I use? Are there other spices I can use to make my food tasty? Is it okay to drink alcohol? How much is allowed? What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be around other people who smoke? Should I measure my blood pressure at home? What type of device should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to measure my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot measure my blood pressure myself, where can I have it measured? What is the ideal reading for my blood pressure? Should I rest before measuring my blood pressure? When should I call my healthcare provider? What is my cholesterol level? Do I need to take medications for it? Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? **High Blood Pressure - What You Should Ask Your Doctor** When your heart pumps blood to the arteries, the pressure of the blood on the walls of the arteries is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is expressed by two numbers: systolic blood pressure over diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest pressure of the blood during the cycle of your heartbeats. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure rises a lot, it puts extra effort on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure remains high all the time, you will be at higher risk of having heart attacks and other vascular diseases, strokes, kidney diseases, and other health problems. Below are questions you may wish to ask the health care provider to help you in caring for your blood pressure. **Questions** **How can I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure?** * What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it okay to eat something not heart-healthy sometimes? What are some ways to eat healthy food when I go to a restaurant? * Do I need to limit the amount of salt I use? Are there other spices I can use to make my food taste delicious? * Is drinking alcohol allowed? What is the allowed amount? * What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be around other people who smoke? **Should I measure my blood pressure at home?** * What type of device should I buy? And where can I learn how to use it? * How often do I need to measure my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it with me on my next visit? * If I cannot measure my blood pressure myself, where can I measure it? * How much should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before measuring blood pressure? * When should I contact the health care provider? * What is my cholesterol level? Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? Back-Translation (to English):

# Type 2 Diabetes - What You Should Ask Your Doctor About Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes high blood sugar (glucose) levels. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage caused by diabetes, and improve your life. Here are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you care for your diabetes. ## Questions Ask your healthcare provider to examine the nerves, skin, and pulse in your feet. Also ask these questions: - How often should I examine my feet? What should I do when examining them? What problems should I call my healthcare provider about? - Who should trim my toenails? Is it acceptable for me to trim them myself? - How should I care for my feet daily? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? - Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your healthcare provider about exercising, including: - Before I start, do I need a heart exam? Eyes? Feet? - What type of exercise program should I follow? What types of activities should I avoid? - When should I check my blood sugar when exercising? What should I carry with me when exercising? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medications when exercising? When should an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) examine my eyes next time? What eye problems should I contact the eye doctor about? Ask your healthcare provider about meeting a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: - What foods raise my blood sugar more than others? - What foods can help me achieve my weight loss goals? Ask your healthcare provider about your diabetes medications: - When should I take them? - What should I do if I miss a dose? - Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What level is too low? What level is too high? What should I do if...

Original English:

Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when the blood sugar (glucose) level is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who take insulin or some other medications to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause serious symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent it. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level that is less than 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is considered low and can harm you. A blood sugar level that is less than 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) requires immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and take any of the following diabetes medications: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at greater risk for low blood sugar if you have had episodes of low blood sugar previously. Recognizing low blood sugar Learn how to know when your blood sugar starts to drop. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy or nervous or anxious Feeling irritable Difficulty thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or strong heartbeat Sometimes blood sugar may be very low even if you do not have symptoms. If it drops too much, you may: Lose consciousness Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time lose the ability to feel low blood sugar. This condition is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar starts to drop too low to help prevent symptoms. Check blood sugar frequently Talk with your health care provider about when you should check your blood sugar daily. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more frequently. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking insulin or diabetes ...

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. Back-Translation (to English):

Low Blood Sugar - Self-Care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who take insulin or certain other medicines to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called "hypoglycemia" (Hypoglycemia). A blood sugar level less than 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is considered low and can harm you. A blood sugar level less than 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a reason to take immediate action. You are at risk of low blood sugar if you have diabetes and take any of the following diabetes medicines: * Insulin * Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had low blood sugar levels previously. Recognizing low blood sugar Know how to distinguish when blood sugar begins to drop. Symptoms include: * Weakness or feeling tired * Shaking (tremors) * Sweating * Headache * Hunger * Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious * Feeling moody or irritable * Difficulty thinking clearly * Double or blurry vision * Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes blood sugar may be very low even if you do not have symptoms. If it drops very significantly, you may: * Pass out (faint) * Have a seizure * Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to feel low blood sugar. This condition is called "hypoglycemia unawareness". Ask the health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitoring device and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar drops significantly to help prevent symptoms. Item: EVAL_0160 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma â€“ Quick-relief medicines Quick-relief medicines for asthma work quickly to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you cough, or you produce wheezing in the chest, or you experience difficulty breathing, or when you are exposed to an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called â€œbronchodilatorsâ€ because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (the bronchi). You and the health care provider can set up a plan for the quick-relief medicines that suit you. This plan will include when you should take them and the dose amount that you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure that you do not run out of the medicine. Bring with you a sufficient amount of the medicine when you travel. Short-acting beta agonists Short-acting beta agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks, and they are considered bronchodilators. They can be used right before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this allows you to breathe better during the attack. Tell the health care provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. The asthma may not be under control, and the health care provider may need to change the dose of the daily control medicines. Some quick-relief medicines for asthma include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline These short-acting beta agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeat. Call the health care provider right away if this side effect happens to you. Headache. Insomnia or difficulty calming down. Item: EVAL_0161 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model A

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma - Quick-Relief Medications Quick-acting asthma medications work rapidly to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you experience coughing, wheezing, difficulty breathing, or an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medications. Many of these medications are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your healthcare provider can develop a plan for the quick-relief medications that are right for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you don't run out of medication. Bring enough medication with you when traveling. Short-Acting Beta Agonists Short-acting beta agonists are the most common quick-relief medications for treating asthma attacks and are considered bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercise to help prevent exercise-induced asthma symptoms. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this allows you to breathe better during an attack. Tell your healthcare provider if you are using quick-relief medications twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your healthcare provider may need to change your dose of daily control medications. Some quick-acting asthma medications include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your healthcare provider immediately if you develop this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your healthcare provider may prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that does not go away. These are medications you take by mouth in the form of pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medications but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your ...

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Back-Translation (to English):

Asthma - Fast-Acting Medicines Fast-acting asthma medicines work quickly to control asthma symptoms. You take these medicines when you suffer from coughing, or wheezing, or difficulty in breathing, or when an asthma attack occurs. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of the airways (bronchi). You can make a plan with your health care provider regarding the fast-acting medicines appropriate for you. This plan will include when you must take them and the amount that must be taken. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out of medicine. Carry a sufficient amount of medicine with you when traveling. Short-Acting Beta-Agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common fast-acting medicines for treating asthma attacks, and they are considered bronchodilators. They can be used immediately before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms resulting from physical exertion. They work by relaxing the muscles of the airways, which allows you to breathe better during the attack. Tell the health care provider if you use fast-acting medicines twice a week or more to control asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and the care provider may need to change the dose of your daily control medicines. Some fast-acting asthma medicines include the following: * Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) * Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) * Metaproterenol * Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause the following side effects: * Anxiety. * Fast and irregular heartbeats. Contact the health care provider immediately if you suffer from this side effect. * Headache. * Restlessness (discomfort). * Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids The health care provider may prescribe oral steroids for you when you suffer from an asthma attack that does not go away. They are medicines you take by mouth in the ...

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Back-Translation (to English):

Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease (COPD) - Questions for Your Doctor Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease (COPD) causes damage to your lungs. This may make it difficult for you to get enough oxygen and expel carbon dioxide from your lungs. Although there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to control your symptoms and improve your quality of life. Here are some questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that may make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help to quit smoking? Will fumes, dust, or keeping pets make my COPD worse? What are the signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should call my healthcare provider? What should I do when I feel like I'm not breathing enough? Am I taking my COPD medications the right way? What medications should I take daily (called controller medications)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? What medications should I take when I feel short of breath (called quick-relief medications or rescue medications)? Is it okay to use these medications daily? What are the side effects of my medications? What side effects should I call my healthcare provider about? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I use an inhalation chamber (spacer)? How will I know when my inhalers are running low? When should I use the nebulizer and when should I use the inhaler? What kind of changes should I make in my home? What kind of changes do I need to make at my work? What shots or vaccines do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all the time? Are there changes in my diet that will help improve my COPD? What do I need to do when I plan to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? What about the airport? What medications should I bring with me? Who should I call if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I can't wal...

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Back-Translation (to English):

Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease (COPD) - What to Ask Your Doctor Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease (COPD) leads to damage to your lungs. This may make it difficult for you to get a sufficient amount of oxygen and get rid of carbon dioxide from your lungs. Although there is no definitive cure for COPD, you can do many things to control symptoms and improve your life. Below are some questions you may wish to ask your health care provider to help you care for your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? * How can I avoid things that might make my condition worse? * How can I prevent getting a lung infection? * How can I get help to quit smoking? * Will fumes, dust, or the presence of pets lead to worsening my condition? * What are the signs indicating that my breathing is getting worse and that I must call the care provider? What should I do when I feel that I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking COPD medications the right way? * What are the medications that I must take every day (called control or controlling medications)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? * Which medications should I take when I suffer from shortness of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medications)? Is it acceptable to use these medications every day? * What are the side effects of my medications? And what are the side effects that call for contacting the care provider? * Am I using the inhaler the right way? Should I use a "spacer"? How will I know when the inhalers are about to run out? * When should I use the nebulizer and when should I use the inhaler? What kind of changes must I make in my home? What kind of changes do I need to make at work? What injections or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If the answer is yes, do I need it at all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help improve my condition? What do I need to do when I plan to travel? * Will I need oxygen on the plane? And what about the airport? * ...

Item: EVAL_0208 | Document: MED_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They are used to treat problems of the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work with less effort by lowering blood pressure. This helps prevent some types of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines are used to treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk of having a stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help prevent the worsening of kidney condition or even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider if you should take these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brand names for ACE inhibitors. Most of them work almost equally well. Side effects may differ from one type to another. Taking your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all your medicines as your healthcare provider told you. See your healthcare provider regularly. The healthcare provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. The healthcare provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time every day. Do not stop taking your medicines without talking to your healthcare provider first. Plan ahead so you do not run out of medicines. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your healthcare provider. Tell your healthcare provider about other medicines you take, including any medicine you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary ...

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are medications used to treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medications make your heart work less hard by lowering blood pressure. This prevents some types of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medications or similar medications. These medications treat high blood pressure and strokes and heart attacks. They may help reduce your risk of having a stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help prevent your kidneys from getting worse or even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider if you should take these medications. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brand names for ACE inhibitors. Most of them work as effectively as the others. Side effects may differ from one type to another. Taking ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all your medications as your healthcare provider told you. Follow up with your healthcare provider regularly. Your healthcare provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medications are working properly. Your healthcare provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medications at the same time every day. Do not stop taking your medications without talking to your healthcare provider first. Plan ahead so you do not run out of your medications. Make sure you have enough of them when traveling. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your healthcare provider. Tell your healthcare provider about other medications you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, or diuretics (water pills), or potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) Inhibitors Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. **How Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) Inhibitors Help** Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are used to treat heart diseases and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work with less effort by lowering blood pressure. And this prevents the worsening of some types of heart diseases. Most people with heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, or strokes, or heart attacks. And they may help in reducing the risk of you having a stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help in preventing the deterioration of your kidneys' condition or even improving their functions, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask the health care provider whether you should take these medicines. **Types of Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) Inhibitors** There are many different names and brand names for Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) inhibitors. And most of them work with the same efficiency. Side effects may vary from one medicine to another. **Taking Your Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) Inhibitors** Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are pills you take by mouth. Take all your medicines as the care provider told you. Follow up with the care provider regularly. The care provider will check your blood pressure and perform blood tests to ensure that the medicines are working correctly. The care provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition to that: * Try to take your medicines at the same time every day. * Do not stop taking your medicines without talking to the care provider first. * Plan ahead so you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when traveling. * Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, tal...

Item: EVAL_0232 | Document: EMER_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use.
