At home, follow your health care providerâ€™s instructions about self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. After your condition became stable, the care providers performed tests and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent another stroke in the future. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common, while you are learning to live with the changes. Depression may appear right after the stroke or even after two years have passed since the stroke. Do not drive your car without permission from your health care provider. Movement and Mobility Moving and doing normal tasks may be difficult after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about changes that can be made at home to make daily activities easier. Learn what you can do to prevent falls and make the bathroom safe to use. Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Back-Translation (to English):

# Stroke - Discharge from the Hospital You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke occurs when blood flow stops to part of the brain. At home, follow your healthcare provider's instructions regarding self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. ## When You Were in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help heal the heart, lungs, and other important organs. After your condition became stable, care providers performed tests and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. ## What to Expect at Home Because of the possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: - Behavior changes - Doing easy tasks - Memory - Moving one side of the body - Muscle spasms - Attention - Sensation or awareness of one part of the body - Swallowing - Speaking or understanding others - Thinking - Vision to one side (hemianopia) You may need help with daily activities that you used to do alone before the stroke. You may undergo physical therapy or occupational therapy to regain your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop a short period after the stroke or even two years after it. **Do not drive a car without permission from your healthcare provider.** ## Getting Around Getting around and doing normal tasks may be difficult after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your healthcare provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make doing daily activities easier. Find out what you can do to prevent falls and keep the bathroom safe during use. Family members and caregivers may need help with: - Exercises to maintain flexibility of elbows, shoulders, and other joints - Monitoring joint stiffness (contractures) - Making sure splints are used the right wa...

It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Item: EVAL_0256 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Care of the Surgical Wound â€“ Open The surgical incision is a cut in the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called the surgical wound. Some incisions are small, and others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you had. Sometimes the incision opens. This may happen along the entire length of the cut or only in part of it. The surgeon may decide not to close it again with stitches (surgical stitches). What to expect at home If the surgeon does not close the wound again with stitches, you will need to care for it at home, because its healing may take time. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. The dressing helps absorb the drainage and prevents the skin from closing before the wound is filled in from the inside. Proper handwashing It is important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol hand sanitizer. Or you can wash your hands by following these steps: - Remove all jewelry from your hands. - Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. - Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing the â€œHappy Birthdayâ€ song or the â€œAlphabet Songâ€ one full time). Clean under your nails as well. - Rinse your hands well. - Dry them with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often you should change the dressing. To prepare for changing the dressing: - Clean your hands before touching the dressing. - Make sure all supplies are within reach. - Prepare a clean work surface. Removing the old dressing: - Gently loosen the adhesive tape from your skin. - Use a clean (non-sterile) medical glove to hold and remove the old dressing. - If the dressing sticks to the wound, moisten it and try again, unless your provider has asked you to remove it dry. - Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. - Clean your hands again after removing the old dressing. Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Surgical Wound Care - Open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, and others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery that was performed on you. Sometimes, the incision opens. This may happen along the entire cut or in only part of it. The surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon did not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, as it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and prevent the skin from closing before the wound fills in from the inside. Proper Hand Washing It is important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based sanitizer. Or you can wash your hands by following these steps: Remove all jewelry from your hands. Wet your hands, directing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing the "Happy Birthday" song or the "Alphabet Song" once completely). Clean under your fingernails as well. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your healthcare provider will tell you how often you should change the dressing. To prepare for changing the dressing: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure all supplies are within reach. Ensure you have a clean work surface. Removing the old dressing: Carefully loosen the adhesive tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to hold the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your healthcare provider instructs you to pull it off dry. Place the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after removing the old dressing. Wound Care You can use a piece of gauze or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use normal saline solutio...

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Surgical Wound Care - Open A surgical incision is a cut in the skin made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, and others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you had. Sometimes, the surgical incision opens. This may happen along the entire cut or in only part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with stitches (sutures). What to expect at home If the surgeon did not close your wound again with stitches, you will need to care for it at home, as it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom up. The dressing helps absorb secretions and prevent the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills up. Washing hands the right way It is important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol cleanser. Or you can wash your hands by following these steps: * Take off all jewelry from your hands. * Wet your hands, pointing them downwards under warm running water. * Apply soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" once). Clean under your fingernails too. * Rinse them well. * Dry them with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing The health care provider will tell you how many times you must change the dressing. To prepare to change the dressing: * Clean your hands before touching the dressing. * Make sure all supplies are within your reach. * Set up a clean work surface. * Remove the old dressing: * Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. * Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. * If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless the care provider directs you to remove it while dry. * Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. * Clean your hands again after removing the old dressing. Wound Care You can use a piece of gauze or a soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound...

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

You will need to be careful so that the artificial hip joint does not come out of place (dislocation of the joint), especially in the first months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that strengthen the new hip joint, and take special precautions. You will need someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center. Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Hip Joint Replacement - Hospital Discharge You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis (artificial limb). This article tells you what you need to do to take care of your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You Are in the Hospital You have undergone hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis (artificial limb). What to Expect at Home By the time you return home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after two to four weeks. Consult your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get in and out of your bed or chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful not to dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for one to two weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help with preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous activity level. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like American football and soccer. But you should be able to do low-impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, and playing tennis and golf. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement - Discharge from the Hospital Surgery was performed to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint called a "prosthesis". This article tells you what you must do to care for your new hip upon leaving the hospital. When you are in the hospital Hip replacement surgery was performed to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a "prosthesis". What to expect at home By the time you return home, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks pass. Check with your surgeon regarding when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only simple help, and be able to get on and off your bed or chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful not to dislocate the artificial hip joint, especially in the first months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for a week to two weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help with preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or sports that require physical contact like American football and soccer. But you should be able to do low-impact activities, such as hiking, gardening (garden works), swimming, playing tennis, and golf. Preparing the home Your bed must be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed must also be high enough for your hips to be higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress must be firm. Remove tripping hazards from your home. * Learn how...

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Heart failure - Leaving the hospital Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to efficiently pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body. When symptoms become acute, staying in the hospital may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital to receive treatment for heart failure. Heart failure occurs when your heart muscles are weak or have difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that transports fluids throughout your body. As is the case with any pump, if the outflow from the pump is not sufficient, the fluids do not move well and accumulate in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluids collect in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team carefully adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you produced. You may have received medications to help your body get rid of excess fluids. You may have had tests done to check how well your heart is working. What to expect at home Your energy will return slowly. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Self-examination at home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up - before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothes each time you weigh yourself. Write your weight every day on a chart so you can track it. Item: EVAL_0302 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medication that makes your blood less prone to forming clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you were told. Changing the way you take warfarin, or taking other medications with it or at different times, and eating certain types of food can change the way warfarin works in your body. If that happens, you may become more prone to forming a clot or suffering from bleeding problems. What to expect at home Warfarin is a medication that makes your blood less prone to forming clots. This may be important if: - You have already had blood clots in your leg, or arm, or heart, or brain. - Your healthcare provider was concerned that a blood clot would form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, or a large heart, or an abnormal heart rhythm, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you take warfarin, you may become more prone to bleeding, even from activities you have always been used to doing. Changing the way you take warfarin, taking other medications, and eating certain types of food can change the way warfarin works in your body. If that happens, you may become more prone to forming a clot or suffering from bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you were told. - Take only the dose prescribed by the healthcare provider. If you miss a dose, contact the healthcare provider for advice. - If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact the healthcare provider or pharmacist immediately. The pills come in different colors, according to the dose. The dose is also written on the pill. - The healthcare provider will check your blood during periodic visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. This test helps ensure that you are taking the appropriate amount of warfarin to help your body. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Back-Translation (to English):

High blood pressure - what you should ask your doctor about When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure that blood exerts on the walls of the arteries is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is expressed by two numbers: systolic blood pressure over diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest pressure of blood during the heartbeat cycle. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure rises excessively, it puts additional pressure on your heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure remains high all the time, you will be at higher risk of heart attacks and other vascular diseases, strokes, kidney diseases, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. **Questions** How can I change my lifestyle to lower blood pressure? What is the healthy diet for the heart? Is it permissible to eat anything unhealthy for the heart sometimes? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to the restaurant? Do I need to limit the amount of salt that I use? Are there other spices I can use to make my food taste good? Is it permissible to drink alcohol? What is the permitted quantity? What can I do to quit smoking? Is it permissible to be near other people who smoke? Should I do blood pressure check at home? What type of device should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How many times do I need to check blood pressure? Should I write the results and bring them to my next visit? If I cannot check blood pressure by myself, where can I check it? What should blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before measuring blood pressure? When should I contact the healthcare provider? What is my cholesterol level? Do I need to take medications for it? Is it permissible to be sexually active? Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hypoglycemia - Self-care Hypoglycemia is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) level is lower than the normal rate. Hypoglycemia may occur in people with diabetes who take insulin or certain other medications to control their diabetes. Hypoglycemia can cause serious symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of hypoglycemia and how to prevent them. What is hypoglycemia? Hypoglycemia is called diabetic deficiency (hypoglycemia). A blood sugar level lower than 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is considered low and can harm you. A blood sugar level lower than 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is considered a reason for immediate action. You are at risk of developing hypoglycemia if you have diabetes and take any of the following diabetes medications: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of developing hypoglycemia if you have previously suffered from low blood sugar levels. Recognizing hypoglycemia Learn how to know when your blood sugar is low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Trembling Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uncomfortable, nervous, or anxious Feeling irritable Difficulty thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or strong heartbeats Your blood sugar may be very low sometimes even if you do not show symptoms. If it drops too much, you may: Lose consciousness Suffer a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to feel hypoglycemia. This is called lack of awareness of hypoglycemia (hypoglycemic unawareness). Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitoring device and its sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar drops too much to help you prevent symptoms. Check your blood sugar frequently Talk to your care provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who suffer from hypoglycemia need to check their blood sugar more freque...

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma - Quick Relief Medications Quick relief medications for asthma work quickly to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you cough, or feel wheezing in the chest, or suffer from difficulty breathing, or go through an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medications. Many of these medications are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of the airways (bronchi). You and the healthcare provider can put a plan for the quick-acting medications that suit you. This plan will include when you should take them and the dose amount you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure that the medication does not run out from you. Take with you a sufficient amount of medication when traveling. Short-acting beta agonists Short-acting beta agonists are considered the most common quick relief medications for treating asthma attacks, and are classified as bronchodilators. They can be used before exercising directly to help prevent asthma symptoms resulting from exercise. These medications work on relaxing the muscles of the airways, which helps you breathe better during an attack. Tell the healthcare provider if you are using quick relief medications twice a week or more to control asthma symptoms. The asthma may not be under control, and the care provider may need to change the dose of daily control medications. Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart diseases and high blood pressure. These medications make your heart work with less effort by lowering blood pressure. Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

Brain stroke - Leaving the hospital You were in the hospital after you suffered a brain stroke. A brain stroke occurs when blood flow stops to a part of the brain. Follow at home the instructions of your health care provider regarding self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When you are in the hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help in healing the heart, lungs, and other vital organs. After your condition became stable, your care providers performed examinations and started treatment to help you recover from the brain stroke and prevent the occurrence of a future brain stroke. Perhaps you stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a brain stroke. What can be expected at home Due to the potential injury in the brain from the brain stroke, you may notice problems in: â€¢ Changes in behavior â€¢ Performing simple tasks â€¢ Memory â€¢ Moving one side of the body â€¢ Muscle spasms â€¢ Attention â€¢ Sensation or awareness of a part of the body â€¢ Swallowing â€¢ Speaking or understanding others â€¢ Thinking â€¢ Vision to one side (half blindness) You may need help in daily activities that you used to do alone before the brain stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a brain stroke is somewhat common while you learn to cope with the changes. It may develop soon after the brain stroke or even after two years have passed. Do not drive your car without permission from your care provider. Mobility Mobility and performing normal tasks may be difficult after the brain stroke. Make sure that your home is safe. Ask your care provider or therapist or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to perform daily activities. Find out what you can do to prevent falls and maintain bathroom safety. Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Back-Translation (to English):

Surgical wound care - open The surgical incision is a cut made in the skin during the surgical operation. It is also called the surgical wound. Some surgical incisions are small, and others are long. The size of the surgical incision depends on the type of surgical operation that was performed for you. Sometimes, the surgical incision opens. This may happen along the entire cut or only part of it. The surgeon may decide not to close it again with surgical threads (sutures). What is expected to happen at home If the surgeon did not close your wound again with surgical threads, you need to take care of it at home, because it may take time until it heals. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. The dressing helps absorb secretions and prevent the skin from closing before the wound underneath it fills. Washing hands properly It is important to clean your hands before changing your dressing. You can use an alcohol sanitizer. Or you can wash your hands by following these steps: 1. Remove all jewelry from your hands. 2. Wet your hands while directing them downward under running warm water. 3. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or "the Alphabet Song" once). Clean under your nails as well. 4. Rinse well. 5. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing The healthcare provider will tell you how many times you should change your dressing. To prepare for changing the dressing: 1. Clean your hands before touching the dressing. 2. Make sure you have all supplies within hand's reach. 3. Prepare a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: 1. Loosen the adhesive tape from your skin carefully. 2. Use a clean (non-sterile) medical glove to take the old dressing and pull it. 3. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless the care provider has authorized you to pull it dry. 4. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Item: EVAL_0382 | Document: SURG_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Joint Replacement - Leaving the Hospital You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This document tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. During Your Stay in the Hospital You have undergone hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What Is Expected to Happen at Home By the time you return home, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without needing significant assistance. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Consult your surgeon regarding when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only slight assistance and be able to get into your bed or out of it and sit on a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without significant assistance. You will need to be careful not to dislocate your artificial hip joint (dislocation), especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help with preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and performing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as skiing on slopes or contact sports like American football and soccer. But you should be able to practice low-impact activities, such as walking long distances, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golf. Preparing the Home Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hip is higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but...
